<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer6461850353033965493">Winner of the 1947 Pulitzer Prize, All the King's Men is one of the most famous and widely read works in American fiction. Its original publication by Harcourt catapulted author Robert Penn Warren to fame and made the novel a bestseller for many seasons. Set in the 1930s, it traces the rise and fall of demagogue Willie Stark, a fictional Southern politician who resembles t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6461850353033965493">Winner of the 1947 Pulitzer Prize, All the King's Men is one of the most famous and widely read works in American fiction. Its original publication by Harcourt catapulted author Robert Penn Warren to fame and made the novel a bestseller for many seasons. Set in the 1930s, it traces the rise and fall of demagogue Willie Stark, a fictional Southern politician who resembles the real-life Huey "Kingfish" Long of Louisiana. Stark begins his career as an idealistic man of the people, but he soon becomes corrupted by success, caught between dreams of service and a lust for power.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6461850353033965493">...more</a>

      </div>

  <a href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten" class="left imgcol" title="Jeffrey Keeten"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1301087352p2/3427339.jpg" alt="Jeffrey Keeten" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/348343920?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 05, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten" class="user" title="Jeffrey Keeten">Jeffrey Keeten</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jeffrey by: </span>
      On the Southern Literary Trail
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3427339-jeffrey-keeten?shelf=southern" class="actionLinkLite">southern</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer348343920">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9310028905233763923"><strong>"Man is conceived in sin and born in corruption and he passeth from the stink of the didie to the stench of the shroud."</strong><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1183.photobucket.com/albums/x462/jkeeten/?action=view&amp;current=RobertPennWarren.jpg"> <img class="escapedImg" alt="Photobucket" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380380772i/737747._SY540_.jpg" /></a><br /><strong>Robert Penn Warren</strong><br /><br />Robert Penn Warren is the only person to win the Pulitzer prize for fiction as well as poetry. He won the prize for fiction in 1946 for this very book. If you are lucky enough to have a great aunt who reads, and bought a lot of books in the 1940s, you might take a gander at her books some time and see if she has a first edition, first printing of this book in</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9310028905233763923"><strong>"Man is conceived in sin and born in corruption and he passeth from the stink of the didie to the stench of the shroud."</strong><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1183.photobucket.com/albums/x462/jkeeten/?action=view&amp;current=RobertPennWarren.jpg">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="Photobucket" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380380772i/737747._SY540_.jpg" /></a><br /><strong>Robert Penn Warren</strong><br /><br />Robert Penn Warren is the only person to win the Pulitzer prize for fiction as well as poetry. He won the prize for fiction in 1946 for this very book. If you are lucky enough to have a great aunt who reads, and bought a lot of books in the 1940s, you might take a gander at her books some time and see if she has a first edition, first printing of this book in her library. <br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1183.photobucket.com/albums/x462/jkeeten/?action=view&amp;current=ATKM1946.jpg">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="Photobucket" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380380772i/737748.jpg" /></a><br /><strong>First edition, First printing of the 1946 edition</strong><br /><br />Depending on the condition of the dust jacket a true first will bring anywhere from $2,000 to $7,000. It will be up to you; if you decide to "liberate" the book, tucking it under your shirt, and sneaking it out with the paper bag of home made oatmeal cookies she always sends you home with. If you are not a natural felon you might just say "hey auntie couldn't you tuck this in a safety deposit box and put my name on it". <br /><br />The last time I was in New Orleans they were shooting the new movie version of <em>All the King's Men</em>. We sat in a little cafe across from where they were setting up a shoot hoping for a glimpse of one of the marquee actors involved in the production. No luck, just film crew people bustling around trying to build a street scene. We were anxious to explore the little bookshops and artist galleries in the French Quarter, so we left before seeing anything truly interesting. I have not seen the 1949 or 2006 film versions. From the reviews I skimmed, both movies seem to struggle to capture the true essence of the book. I'm not surprised, even if they put the book through a small holed strainer, they would still have way more material than what a standard length movie can handle. <br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1183.photobucket.com/albums/x462/jkeeten/?action=view&amp;current=AlltheKingsMen1949.jpg">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="Photobucket" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380380772i/737749.jpg" /></a><br /><strong>1949 Movie Poster</strong><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1183.photobucket.com/albums/x462/jkeeten/?action=view&amp;current=AlltheKingsMen2006.jpg">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="Photobucket" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380380772i/737750._SY540_.jpg" /></a><br /><strong>2006 Movie Poster</strong><br /><br />Jack Burden, newspaper reporter, finds himself following around an ambitious, well meaning, but naive candidate named Willie Stark. A man hand picked to split the vote in the primary and insure the nomination of the customary corrupt, crony, politician that Louisiana is famous for. Stark is the only person who is unaware that the fix is on. He is stumping and receiving discouraging indifference from his crowds as he tries to tell them the <strong>truth</strong>. As he finds himself on the ropes more than he is in the ring, he starts to understand that to be successful he will have to give the crowds what they want. He replaces substance with hyperbole, and Burden observes the emergence of a candidate and the corruption of an honest man. Warren based Stark on the dynamic personage of Louisiana governor Huey P. Long. <br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1183.photobucket.com/albums/x462/jkeeten/?action=view&amp;current=HueyPLong.jpg">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="Photobucket" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380380772i/737751.jpg" /></a><br /><strong>Huey P. Long</strong><br /><br />Burden soon finds himself unemployed, but Stark always liked him and gave him a prominent position on his staff. Stark, though soundly defeated, uses the time between elections to become a polished orator and electable candidate. Burden studied for a history degree in college and believes from his studies that truth will always win out. As he becomes more ensnared in the shady activities of Governor Stark's administration he starts to stumble over his own high ideas of the worthiness of truth. He tries to convince himself that he just does what the boss wants him to do. What the boss does with the information he brings him has nothing to do with him, but the longer he is involved, and the more people he knows who become victims of Stark's ambition the less distance he can claim.<br /><br /><em>"I didn't mean to cause any ruckus. I didn't think--" And all the while that cold, unloving part of the mind--that maiden aunt, that washroom mirror the drunk stares into, that still small voice, that maggot in the chess of your self-esteem, that commentator on the ether nightmare, that death's-head of lipless rationality at your every feast--all that while that part of the mind was saying: You're making it worse, your lying is just making it worse, can't you shut up, you blabbermouth!"</em><br /><br />Burden is in love with Anne Stanton, his childhood friend and the daughter of a previous governor. Briefly they are an item and then they drift apart. Burden marries Lois, the woman who has the <em>"peach bloom of cheeks, the pearly ripe but vigorous bosom, the supple midriff, the brooding, black, velvety-liquid eyes, the bee-stung lips, the luxurious thighs."</em> Despite these attributes they have different goals and different ambitions and the elephant in the room is the fact that Jack is still in love with Anne. He becomes close friends with Anne again. He can't help but make allusions to the fact that his marriage proposal is still on the table. Even though she is 35 and never been married she continues to dance around the issue. Burden can't ever see her as just a friend.<br /><br /><em>"It was Anne Stanton herself, who stood there in the cool room of the looking glass, above the bar barricade of bright bottles and siphons across some distance of blue carpet, a girl--well, not exactly a girl any more, a young woman about five-feet-four with the trimmest pair of nervous ankles and smallish hips which, however, looked as round as though they had been turned on a lathe, and a waist just the width to make you wonder if you could span it with your hand, and all of this done up in a swatch of gray flannel which pretended to a severe mannish cut but actually did nothing but scream for attention to some very unmannish arrangements within."</em><br /><br />Stark still sees himself as one of the good guys despite the number of men he has felt compelled to destroyed. He came to the conclusion that it was better to destroy them than to bribe them. If he bribes them he still has to keep those untrustworthy associates in his organization. If he destroys them they can no longer thwart his ambitious aims. He is on a self-imposed mission to use the corrupt system, but use it for good.<br /><br /><em>"Goodness. Yeah, just plain, simple goodness. Well you can't inherit that from anybody. You got to make it. If you want it. And you got to make it out of badness. Badness. And you know why? Because there isn't anything else to make it out of."</em><br /><br />When Burden experiences the ultimate betrayal it hit me like a left hook coming out of the smokey darkness of an Oklahoma bar. I never saw it coming and I had to stagger away from the book for a while. Jack took 8 days and ran away to California. I took thirty minutes to go stand out on my deck and let some fresh air sort my scattered thoughts.<br /><br />There is a whole marvelous section on Cass Mastern, Jack's relative, who provides a colorful history for Jack to research for his PHD. I almost need a separate review to handle the intricate betrayals explored by Warren in that section. I notice that the departure from the main story line bothered other reviewers. I just thought I'd been handed another thick seam of gold to be mined. I like history and I especially like family history, so I didn't mind the story in the story at all. <br /><br />Political cynicism wrapped in lyrical prose makes this one of the more fascinating books I've read in many, many years. It is an honest book, exposing all the worst elements of human behavior. We are so good at fooling ourselves into thinking that when we do wrong for the greater good we are still on the side of the angels. Highly recommended!!<br /><br />If anyone has any political novels that they love, and feel I should read, please send me your recommendations. <br /><br />If you wish to see more of my most recent book and movie reviews, visit <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.jeffreykeeten.com" target="_blank">http://www.jeffreykeeten.com</a><br />I also have a Facebook blogger page at: <a rel="nofollow" href="https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten" target="_blank">https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten</a></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9310028905233763923">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a href="/user/show/21021705-t-moore" style="font-weight:bold">T Moore</a>

      
<span id="freeTextContainer10771839787761771127">Jeffery: You sure identified my quote of the book in your opening. There are SO many too. Beautiful and mind opening written stuff here - page after p</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10771839787761771127">Jeffery: You sure identified my quote of the book in your opening. There are SO many too. Beautiful and mind opening written stuff here - page after page after page.<br /><br />Surprised by the lack of comments in the reviews of the writer's seeming acceptance of the racism and Jim Crow. (I am not saying here, he championed it - Ala, Mitchell does in her "Gone With The Wind"). <br /><br />However, he did not blink when writing about the men and women of his time and their racist beliefs. Were these views his at that time or did he just acquiesce to all of this as a way of life? I am left with no clear idea. Other than, I would expect that he didn't - given some of the clues he leaves us in the book ( I'm not looking for a follow up author's bio here - it is not important). <br /><br />I wrote it off to realism. That said, ATKM with most books about the South's power elite leave me troubled regarding the region's great stain on our land's racist condition - even into its art. <br /><br />Sometimes, "realism" must be damned and some truth told beyond it to have real virtue - IMO. I accept that it was written in 1946 and read it with a large dose of salt. I would only point out, realism had not stopped other American authors from objecting and calling attention to racism in America literature prior to this book's writing. I think, he could have done more is all.<br /><br />I enjoyed the handling of our protagonist's, Jack Burden, privileged nature. It was as well done as it could be given the nature of our elitist country and its art. He clearly went to some effort to avoid glamorizing the elite and their lifestyle.<br /><br />I had resisted reading the book for years. Mostly because of the subject matter and it's treatment of Huey Long based on the movie, you posted. I was wrong to have waited so long to read it.<br /><br />It is certainly one of the great works of American literature by any standard. That it matches my leftist views or ideas should never have been a matter that prevented my reading of it. <br /><br />As they say, "Better late than never". <br /><br />It is as near to perfection as a long novel can be - even his Cass digression worked. Digressions have ruined or at least unnecessarily cluttered and weighted down many a great book. Hugo always springs to mind for me here. His Sewers of Paris and Battle of Waterloo digressions in Les Miserables - YIKES!!! Must admit, I was worried for a bit when Warren took this turn in Chapter 4 - HAHA <br /><br />As the great American "Political" novel, it will always be the great standard. Cannot see it being challenged ever.<br /><br />A good review and a correct GR rating of 5 plus Stars.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_163010525">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/163010525" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5300620-steve-sckenda" class="left imgcol" title="Steve Sckenda"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1409027039p2/5300620.jpg" alt="Steve Sckenda" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/163010525?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 29, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5300620-steve-sckenda" class="user" title="Steve Sckenda">Steve Sckenda</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/5527.All_the_King_s_Men" title="Paperback 9780156004800" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Those Intrigued by Politics, the South, or Sin</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5300620-steve-sckenda?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5300620-steve-sckenda?shelf=politics" class="actionLinkLite">politics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5300620-steve-sckenda?shelf=fathers-and-sons" class="actionLinkLite">fathers-and-sons</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5300620-steve-sckenda?shelf=pulitzer-prize" class="actionLinkLite">pulitzer-prize</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5300620-steve-sckenda?shelf=time-100" class="actionLinkLite">time-100</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer163010525">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3603846920872170845"><strong> <em>“Is you is, or is you ain’t my constituency?”</em> </strong> <br />(O Brother Where Art Thou? Coen Brothers’ film, 2000)<br /><br />Do corrupt and greedy humans beget corrupt and greedy politicians? Do we get the leaders we deserve? Poet Robert Penn Warren received the Pulitzer Prize for his 1946 masterpiece about a corrupt governor of an unnamed Southern state (resembling Louisiana) during the Great Depression of the 1930’s, but Warren poses difficult questions about the nature of human corruption and the nature of evil that</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3603846920872170845"><strong>
  <em>“Is you is, or is you ain’t my constituency?”</em>
</strong> <br />(O Brother Where Art Thou? Coen Brothers’ film, 2000)<br /><br />Do corrupt and greedy humans beget corrupt and greedy politicians? Do we get the leaders we deserve? Poet Robert Penn Warren received the Pulitzer Prize for his 1946 masterpiece about a corrupt governor of an unnamed Southern state (resembling Louisiana) during the Great Depression of the 1930’s, but Warren poses difficult questions about the nature of human corruption and the nature of evil that snake up the Mississippi River into the American heart of darkness. <br /><br />Governor Willie Stark (based on Huey P. Long) once used populism to serve poor workers and farmers to whom he brought electricity, roads, schools, and hospitals. His noble aspirations derailed, Stark then used his oratorical power and psychological insight to enrich himself and his cronies. <br /><br />Jack Burden, the first-person narrator (whose inner idealism clashes with his façade of cynicism) receives a moral education at the hands of Willie. Jack passively surrenders his honor and idealism to become a hatchet man for Willie Stark—much to the shame of his aristocratic family. Will Jack develop from a passive and cynical character into morally responsible free agent? <br /><br />“All the King’s Men” explores the mystery of humanity that defies formula or dogma. Some of these characters cannot handle the moral ambiguity or the ghosts of history that haunt the South. <br />Jack’s father, Ellis Burden, “the Scholarly Attorney,” has retreated into religion and pronounced,<em> “I will not touch the world of foulness again.” </em>(202) <br /><br />Another approach to the mystery of iniquity is exhibited by Adam Stanton, an idealistic doctor who applies the scientific method to humanity: <blockquote> But Adam, <strong>he is a scientist, and everything is tidy for him, and one molecule of oxygen always behaves the same way when it gets around two molecules of hydrogen, and a thing is always what it is, and so when Adam the romantic makes a picture of the world in his head, it is just like the picture of the world Adam the scientist works with. All tidy. All neat. The molecule of good always behaves the same way. The molecule of bad always behaves the same way.</strong> (248)</blockquote> We see how the pragmatic “man of fact” (Willie) collides with the idealistic “man of ideas” (Adam).<br /><br />Can a leader’s evil motivations be harnessed on behalf of the commonwealth? Sophocles and Shakespeare dramatized the effects of the sins of kings upon their kingdoms and the effects of the sins of fathers upon their children. Jack, muses: <blockquote>I must believe that Willie Stark was a great man. What happened to his greatness is not the question. Perhaps he spilled it on the ground the way you spill a liquid when the bottle breaks. Perhaps he piled up his greatness and burnt it in one great blaze in the dark like a bonfire and then there wasn’t anything but dark and the embers winking. Perhaps he could not tell his greatness from ungreatness and so mixed them together that what was adulterated was lost. Bust he had it. I must believe that. (427) </blockquote> The American South still subscribes to St. Paul’s belief that all humans are corrupt and that the heart of man is desperately wicked above all things. It is a region well known for its friendly charm, fervid religiosity, and its historic-idealistic cruelty. The atmosphere of Faulkner and John Calvin permeates this novel, which is written in the rhythmic lyricism of King James English. <br /><br />A question plagues theologians: How can an all-good and an all-powerful God create, tolerate, or coexist with evil? Jack answers: <blockquote>The creation of man whom God in his foreknowledge knew doomed to sin was the awful index of God’s omnipotence. For it would have been a thing of trifling and contemptible ease for Perfection to create mere perfection. To do so would…be not creation but extension. Separateness is identity and the only way for God to create…man was to make him separate from God Himself, and to be separate from God is to be sinful. The creation of evil is therefore the index of God’s glory and his power. That had to be so that the creation of good might be the index of man’s glory and power.(437)</blockquote> To return to my introductory thoughts, I have often pondered whether voters are hypocritical whiners when they excoriate the leaders that they themselves choose often for the basest of reasons. Isn’t Willie Stark a byproduct of his own constituents? Willie proclaims, <em>“my study is the heart of people,”</em>(6) which led to his conclusion that <em>
  <strong>“man is conceived in sin and born in corruption and he passeth from the stink of the didie to the stench of the shroud.”</strong>
</em>(49) I think Willie is saying, “you don’t like the looks of me? Look in the mirror.”<br /><br /><em>“The human being is a very complicated contraption and that they are not good or bad but are good and bad and the good comes out of the bad and the bad out of the good.” </em>(248) Willie believes that bad must be done before good can be accomplished and that "goodness" is made out of "badness"--because there isn't anything else out of which to make it. <br /><br />Jack is also on a quest for knowledge and searches for a father, though he <em>“can’t know whether knowledge will save him or kill him.” </em>Burden is haunted by the fear that life is nothing more than the "Great Twitch," (the dance of blood along the arteries and veins) which then evokes a paralysis of conscience that he calls the "Great Sleep." Will Jack outgrow the Great Twitch and avoid the Great Sleep? <em>“Each of us is the son of a million fathers.”</em> (436) Will Jack find and come to live in his father’s house? Will the discovery of truth rob him of the past and kill his father? Will the truth give the past back to Jack? <br /><br />My "Conclusion"<br />This is one of my favorite books, which I have read three times and hope to re-read because I want to avoid “the Great Sleep.” I ask questions as I continue to search for books that help me to see the world and humans in all their glorious complexity. Readers don’t settle for simple formulas or dogmas. We know that people do not behave predictably, like molecules. Readers recognize the complexity and ask the right questions--even if answers forever elude us.<br /><br />November 5, 2012</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3603846920872170845">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_16687444">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/16687444" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/954812-heather" class="left imgcol" title="Heather"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1279752265p2/954812.jpg" alt="Heather" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/16687444?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 01, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/954812-heather" class="user" title="Heather">Heather</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/5527.All_the_King_s_Men" title="Paperback 9780156004800" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/954812-heather?shelf=literary-novels" class="actionLinkLite">literary-novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer16687444">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11911053553637829082">Compelling, overstuffed, overplotted, sexist, labyrinthine, poetic, atmospheric. To me this book's status as The Great American Political Novel seems like a terrific bitter joke, because the author's vision of "politics" is comprised entirely of blackmail, physical intimidation, pork-barreling, rabble-rousing, nepotism, bribery, rigged elections, and hilariously contrived "family values" photo shoots. (I love the scene where a photographer and two aides attempt to wrestle a comatose, foul-smelli</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11911053553637829082">Compelling, overstuffed, overplotted, sexist, labyrinthine, poetic, atmospheric. To me this book's status as The Great American Political Novel seems like a terrific bitter joke, because the author's vision of "politics" is comprised entirely of blackmail, physical intimidation, pork-barreling, rabble-rousing, nepotism, bribery, rigged elections, and hilariously contrived "family values" photo shoots. (I love the scene where a photographer and two aides attempt to wrestle a comatose, foul-smelling dog into position for a shot of the dog leaping up to greet its beloved master, the Governor!)<br /><br />I would place this novel in the Philosophical Potboiler genre (together with "East of Eden" and "Sophie's Choice"). There are lengthy meditations on the Human Condition, the nature of History, the problem of Free Will, the Original Sin of slavery as a hereditary taint corrupting the southern upper class, etc... woven among scenes featuring such archetypes as the Angelic Woman (Anne Stanton), the Demonic Woman (Sadie Burke, and Cass Mastern's mistress), the Saintly Aesthete and Crypto-Homosexual (Adam Stanton), the Seductive Mother, the "Colonel Sanders" With a Secret (Judge Irwin), and What's Bred in the Bone Coming Out in the Flesh (Tom Stark, the embodiment of his father's egoism and brutality). In the ranks of minor characters we find the Long-Suffering Wife in the Country (Mrs. Stark), and Flannery O'Connor-style Mad Missionary (Jack's father). What an array!<br /><br />There are a number of rather heavy-handed themes, of which I thought the most interesting was the contrast between Jack the self-identified "student of history" and product of History, and Willie the man without a history... no family, no formal education, no tradition, nothing to explain his ambition, charisma, ruthlessness, and power over others. There seems to be a trade-off between History and Act. Jack is Burdened by the past at every level -- his parents' broken marriage, his half-mad father, his unfinished dissertation, the end of the plantation class's reign in Southern politics, the guilt of slavery. He lives in a fog of depression, cynicism, sophistication, and rationalization. He is fascinated by Willie at their first meeting because Willie is his opposite: an earnest rube who seems unaware of his own dorkiness and believes the political game could and should be played fair. <br /><br />But Willie isn't just a naif. He's also a kind of monster. Even at that early stage there's a monstrous ego and ambition germinating inside him... ambition not for political goals but for personal power and domination. Where does his ambition come from? What sets Willie apart from any other impoverished child of dirt farmers in any other wretched little town like Mason City? And which side is the true Willie Stark -- the idealist who fights on behalf of poor farmers and families, the builder of new roads and schools and hospitals, or the bully who fights for the sadistic joy of humiliating and dominating others? <br /><br />These mysteries haunt the novel, and Penn Warren never offers a solution. Willie remains an enigma from start to finish. In fact I felt that Penn Warren wrote himself into a corner - he COULDN'T solve the enigma of Willie Stark's origins and essential nature, so he shifted focus to the more solvable mysteries in Jack Burden's past.<br /><br />I don't think the Jack Burden plot has aged particularly well. It has the kind of heavy-handed Freudianism you see in 1950s movies... the seductive mother, the discovery of the True Father; the taboo of virginity; the sorting of women into angels and whores, spirits and bodies. Almost every character has at least one light or dark "double" (Willie/Jack, Willie/Adam, Jack/Adam, Sadie/Ann, Burden/Irwin, Lois/Ann, etc), which is very schematic. The happy ending is only achieved by the death/disappearance of everyone but Jack and Ann... they don't so much overcome or escape the Burden of history as have history conveniently relax its grip on them. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11911053553637829082">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer82892503">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7038968335997562599"><strong>All the King's Men: Robert Penn Warren's Spider Web</strong><br /><br /><em>This Novel was chosen as a group read by members of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/63470-on-the-southern-literary-trail">On the Southern Literary Trail</a> for July 2012 and again in October,2014.</em><br /><br /><blockquote> <em>"It all began, as I have said, when the Boss, sitting in the black Cadillac which sped through the night, said to me (to Me who was what Jack Burden, the student of history, had grown up to be) "There is always something."<br />And I said, "Maybe not on the Judge."<br />And he said, "Man is conceived in sin and born in corruption and h</em></blockquote></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7038968335997562599"><strong>All the King's Men: Robert Penn Warren's Spider Web</strong><br /><br /><em>This Novel was chosen as a group read by members of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/63470-on-the-southern-literary-trail">On the Southern Literary Trail</a> for July 2012 and again in October,2014.</em><br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>"It all began, as I have said, when the Boss, sitting in the black Cadillac which sped through the night, said to me (to Me who was what Jack Burden, the student of history, had grown up to be) "There is always something."<br />And I said, "Maybe not on the Judge."<br />And he said, "Man is conceived in sin and born in corruption and he passeth from the stink of the didie to the stench of the shroud. There is always something."</em>
</blockquote><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1151.photobucket.com/user/mmcsullivan/media/all_the_kings_men1_zps42b3871e.jpg.html">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt=" photo all_the_kings_men1_zps42b3871e.jpg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1412563855i/11391544.jpg" /></a><br /><strong>
  <em>There is always something, even on the Judge. Broderick Crawford as Willie Stark, John Ireland as Jack Burden, and Adam Greenleaf as Judge Stanton from the 1949 film. The film changed the identity of Judge Irwin to Judge Stanton. A slight problem with the object of Jack's romance.&gt;</em>
</strong><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1151.photobucket.com/albums/o631/mmcsullivan/?action=view&amp;current=AllTheKingsMen.jpg">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="Photobucket" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380330182i/692322.jpg" /></a><br /><br /><em>First Edition, Harcourt Brace &amp; Jovanovich, 1946</em><br /><br />If you're expecting a fictional recounting that serves as a short cut to T. Harry William's masterful biography of Huey Long this isn't it.<br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1151.photobucket.com/albums/o631/mmcsullivan/?action=view&amp;current=Kingfish.jpg">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="Photobucket" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380330182i/692323.jpg" /></a><br /><br />But Williams does have something to say that pointedly echoes the themes Robert Penn Warren wove into a masterpiece of American politics.<br /><br /><blockquote>
<em>"I believe that some men, men of power, can influence the course of history. They appear in response to conditions, but they may alter the conditions, may give a new direction to history. In the process they may do great good or evil or both, but whatever the case they leave a different kind of world behind them."</em>, p.ix, Preface, <a title="T. Harry Williams" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/85312.T__Harry_Williams">T. Harry Williams</a>, <a title="Huey Long by T. Harry Williams" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/239356.Huey_Long">Huey Long</a>,Alfred A. Knopf, 1969.</blockquote><br /><br />That Willie Stark is a stand in for Huey Long, <a title="Robert Penn Warren" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3736.Robert_Penn_Warren">Robert Penn Warren</a> frankly admits. I was fortunate to find the Thirty-Fifth Edition of the novel, published in 1981. It contained a new, and very informative introduction by Warren.<br /><br />Warren did not originally envision this work as a novel, but as a tragic drama entitled "Proud Flesh." Warren ended up putting that manuscript away. He realized that he had focused on a man of power rather than those few people who are always surrounding that man of power, and in writing <a title="All the King's Men by Robert Penn Warren" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5527.All_the_King_s_Men">All the King's Men</a>, Warren focused on the "Greek" chorus to whom he had not given proper voice in his originally conceived work.<br /><br />So, there we have the title, "All the King's Men," the chorus that relates the rise and fall of Willie Stark. For all great men have an inner circle, some of whom are as vague as phantoms, performing the will of the King and they will perform that will whether it be good or evil. But all the King's Men cannot put Humpty Dumpty together again should the King fall.<br /><br />Warren proposes the question of whether those minions are mere pawns or whether they recognize the consequences of their actions and accept responsibility for them, and if so, can they find redemption for the evil they do, even when it is couched in terms of doing good. Willie Stark, the Boss, is a practical man. So, politics is a dirty business. He tells us, <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>"Dirt's a funny thing, come to think of it, there ain't a thing but dirt on this green God's globe except what's under water, and that's dirt too. It's dirt makes the grass grow. A diamond ain't a thing in the world but a piece of dirt that got awful hot. And God-a-Mighty picked up a handful of dirt and blew on it and made you and me and George Washington and mankind blessed in faculty and apprehension. It all depends on what you do with the dirt."&gt;</em>
</blockquote><br /><br />Jack Burden is a one man Greek Chorus that tells us the story of Willie Stark. And it is Jack Burden who provides the moral center of the novel. In one long narrative voice, Jack, a child of privilege, intrigues us relating the present and the past, not only Willie's but his own. Willie's rise is rather straight forward. As Williams tells us in Long's biography, Willie appears on the Louisiana scene in response to conditions of the Great Depression, which seemingly provided the fuel for Populism common to that era.<br /><br />Jack comes from a level of society that comprised the previous leaders of Louisiana, a class who would forever be opposed to a man of Willie Stark's origin and philosophy. He is the friend of Adam and Anne Stanton, the children of the governor preceding Stark. His mentor is Judge Irwin who advised and influenced Jack from his youth. His father, Ellis Burden, the "scholarly lawyer" is a good friend of the Judge. His mother is beautiful, poised, and confident.<br /><br />So, why would Ellis Burden walk out of his law office one day to become a street evangelist? But Jack's mother has no problem keeping a stream of husbands in her bed. It's enough to make a fellow a little cynical. Rebellious, too. Rebellious enough to go to State University and study history. <br /><br />Jack has a future. He's working on his doctorate, studying the papers of an ancestor named Cass Mastern. The papers of Mastern serve as a mirror of Jack's life. But Mastern, who betrayed a friend by having a love affair with his friend's wife, lives the rest of his life with the knowledge of that betrayal. It is Cass who writes in his journal,<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>The world is all of one piece. He learned that the world is like an enormous spider web and if you touch it, however lightly, at any point, the vibration ripples to the remotest perimeter and the drowsy spider feels the tingle and is drowsy no more but springs out to fling the gossamer coils about you who have touched the web and then inject the black, numbing poison under your hide. It does not matter whether or not you meant to brush the web of things. Your happy foot or your gay wing may have brushed it ever so lightly, but what happens always happens and there is the spider, bearded black and with his great faceted eyes glittering like mirrors in the sun, or like God’s eye, and the fangs dripping."</em>
</blockquote><br /><br />The long and the short of it is that our actions have consequences and we owe a responsibility for the consequences of our actions. This is a premise that Jack would rather reject.<br /><br />Rather, Jack grasps on to the theory of the "Great Twitch," a world in which the actions of people are no more controllable than the muscles of a frog's leg twitching in response to an electrical impulse. However it is Cass Mastern who was correct. In rejecting his ancestor's journal, Jack becomes the cynical, wisecracking news reporter assigned to cover Willie Stark's first gubernatorial election. It is Jack Burden, along with savvy political advisor Sadie Burke who tell Stark he's been duped into running to split the vote of the opposing candidate to bring about the win by yet another politician.<br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1151.photobucket.com/user/mmcsullivan/media/JackandSadie_zps7f93f79c.jpg.html">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt=" photo JackandSadie_zps7f93f79c.jpg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1412563855i/11391545.jpg" /></a><br /><strong>
  <em>Jack Burden and Sadie Burke telling Willie he's been had.</em>
</strong><br /><br />It is that campaign that transforms not only Willie Stark into a Kingfish lookalike, but transforms Jack into Stark's most trusted fix it man. "Maybe not the Judge." Oh, yes, even the Judge. And so it is that a chain of consequences begins to be unveiled, each the result of a deliberate, undeniable action. <br /><br />Even the death of Willie Stark is a consequence of one of the Boss's improvident decisions. As Warren wrote, <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>"The end of man is knowledge but there's one thing he can't know. He can't know whether knowledge will save him or kill him. He will be killed, all right, but he can't know whether he is killed because of the knowledge which he has got or because of the knowledge which he hasn't got and which if he had it would save him."</em>
</blockquote><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1151.photobucket.com/user/mmcsullivan/media/StarkHospital_zps4ad098cf.jpg.html">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt=" photo StarkHospital_zps4ad098cf.jpg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1412563855i/11391546.jpg" /></a><br /><strong>
  <em>Do the ends justify the means? Can Willie Stark find redemption?</em>
</strong><br /><br />Willie's death comes about, not from an assassin who believes him to be a dictator, but for a very personal reason. Nor will I even resort to a spoiler alert. I'm simply not going to tell you, because I want you to read this book. <br /><br />And what of Jack? I will share the final sentence, and I remind you that Jack is the narrator.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>"Go out of the house and go into the convulsion of the world, out of history into history and the awful responsibility of Time."</em>
</blockquote> <br /><br />Perhaps Jack Burden has come to terms with his ancestor, Cass Mastern.<br /><br />To say this is a masterpiece about American politics is true. But it goes much further than that. It is a reminder that the past is the father of the future. They are inevitably inseparable.<br /><br /><strong>EXTRAS! EXTRAS!</strong><br /><br /><strong>Huey Long: The Man Behind Willie Stark</strong><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=hphgHi6FD8k&amp;list=PLGoQI1Qczqhu-Zn8Ca9fFC9teIc9UceAP">Huey Long's "Share the Wealth Speech" </a><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=KLyfrb15v-Q&amp;list=PLGoQI1Qczqhu-Zn8Ca9fFC9teIc9UceAP&amp;index=3">Huey Long on the Difference between Democrats and Republicans </a><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=0NUvzw4E22g&amp;index=4&amp;list=PLGoQI1Qczqhu-Zn8Ca9fFC9teIc9UceAP"> The Assassination of Huey Long </a><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=AbyMeMApC3U%20%3E%20Paramount%20Newsreel%20of%20Huey%20Long%20Coverage%20%3C/a%3E%3Cbr%20/%3E%3Cbr%20/%3E%3Cb%3ERobert%20Penn%20Warren%20and%20All%20the%20King%27s%20Men%3C/b%3E%3Cbr%20/%3E%3Cbr%20/%3E%3Ca%20href=%20"> A Biographical Documentary of Robert Penn Warren </a><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=bagVGDYP9j4"> Robert Penn Warren, All the King's Men, and Huey Long </a> WARNING CONTAINS SPOILERS<br /><br /><strong>Soundtrack</strong><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=MGs2iLoDUYE">Louisana 1927 by Randy Newman </a><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ZmFQu_8u1TU">Kingfish by Randy Newman </a><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=VzU0Cok3guQ&amp;list=PLt5lR5qt_VGhfiCQpAa-oLQCT9XmVSxMh&amp;index=3"> Every Man a King, written and sung by Huey Long. <br /><br /></a></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_143625206">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/143625206" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/405390-kemper" class="left imgcol" title="Kemper"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1451574879p2/405390.jpg" alt="Kemper" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/143625206?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 06, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/405390-kemper" class="user" title="Kemper">Kemper</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/5527.All_the_King_s_Men" title="Paperback 9780156004800" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=politics" class="actionLinkLite">politics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=100" class="actionLinkLite">100</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=classic-lit" class="actionLinkLite">classic-lit</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=2011" class="actionLinkLite">2011</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer143625206">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12445593338185754662">At first glance, Willie Stark seems like he would have been the perfect Tea Party candidate. He uses fiery rhetoric to stir up crowds by claiming to be just like them and that he’s going to bust the heads of those evil ole politicians at the state house to force them the straighten up and do things the right way. But on the other hand, Willie actually knows something about government and uses his tactics to improve the lives of poor people by taxing the wealthy and using that money to do things</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12445593338185754662">At first glance, Willie Stark seems like he would have been the perfect Tea Party candidate. He uses fiery rhetoric to stir up crowds by claiming to be just like them and that he’s going to bust the heads of those evil ole politicians at the state house to force them the straighten up and do things the right way. But on the other hand, Willie actually knows something about government and uses his tactics to improve the lives of poor people by taxing the wealthy and using that money to do things like improve roads and provide free health care so maybe he wouldn’t fit in with Sarah Palin after all.<br /><br />This classic novel tells the story of Willie Stark through the eyes of Jack Burden. Jack came from a privileged background but eventually turned his back on that life and became a cynical political newspaper reporter in an unnamed corrupt southern state. When Jack first meets Stark, he thinks of him as ’Cousin Willie from the country.’ because of his rube manner. Stark is a smart, hardworking and principled county commissioner, but he gets in over his head when he tries to award a government contract to the actual best bid and the corrupt politicians trash him for it.<br /><br />Then Stark is tricked into running for governor by the state political machine to split the rural vote and make sure that the party favorite wins. Stark had been getting nowhere with his carefully planned speeches that patiently explained needed changes to the tax codes and other government business, but when he finds out he’s been played for a fool, Stark finds his voice as an angry hick who is tired of being abused by the politicians. Using his new populist tactics of playing up his upbringing as a poor farm boy who taught himself law at nights and promises to kick the collective ass of the political good-ole-boy network, Stark eventually does win the governorship, and Jack joins him as his political hatchet man.<br /><br />Stark no longer cares about doing things the right way. He becomes a political force in the state through a combination of bullying, cajoling or bribing anyone who gets in his way. To Willie’s way of thinking, the state is full of sons-of-bitches that he either has to buy or break to get things done, and he is now fully convinced that the ends justify the means. He does actually follow through on his promises to try and help the common people of the state, but many consider him even more dangerous than the corrupt people he’s fighting.<br /><br />Jack has no problems with the way that Willie runs thing until the governor gets angry at the incorruptible Judge Irwin for backing a rival in an election. When Willie can’t charm or bully the Judge into falling into line, he orders Jack to dig up some dirt on the man. However, Jack has known and admired the Judge since childhood so he has reservations about the assignment. Trying to find the Judge’s dirty laundry brings back Jack’s issues with his mother and father, and the girl he loved and lost, Anne Stanton. Things get even stickier when Willie decides that the only man to run his new pet project, a huge modern hospital, is Ann’s brother and Jack’s childhood friend, Adam.<br /><br />I absolutely loved the way that Stark is portrayed in this book. It was inspired by Huey P. Long in Louisiana, a politician who accomplished a lot for the poor of his state but did so with highly questionable methods. Willie does indeed want to protect the common people from the ‘sons-of-bitches’ who have let the state wallow in poverty and neglect while lining their pockets, but this isn’t a simple case of power corrupting either. Willie always had a lot of ambitions for his political career, and he tried to play it straight at first because he thought that‘s how it was done. Once he saw the ugliness of reality behind the scenes, Willie seemingly adopts the same tactics without a second thought. Power didn’t change Willie, he changed to get and keep power, and he seems to relish his opportunities to take revenge on the types who screwed him over early in his career. <br /><br />Warren’s prose is elegant and lyrical. He brings an entire region alive with a cast that includes everyone from the high society to the poorest farmers. His descriptions are so good that you can almost feel the humidity and hear the insects at times. However, he did tend to go on a bit long for my taste when relaying Jack’s personal history and insights. I would have liked more of Willie laying on the charm or ruthlessly taking down an opponent.<br /><br />They say that watching government work is like watching sausage get made. Everyone wants the finished product, but no one wants to see how it‘s done. This story gives weight to this idea. It’s something that will make any reader think about whether one can get anything done in a democracy without deals being cut or threats being made. Even if the goal is accomplished, is the whole thing tainted because of how it came about? And how can a person with even the best of intentions work in a system like this without becoming corrupted? </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12445593338185754662">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/73029750?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 14, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/399189-weinz" class="user" title="Weinz">Weinz</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/5527.All_the_King_s_Men" title="Paperback 9780156004800" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Weinz by: </span>
      Bernie
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/399189-weinz?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer73029750">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5072401516128592987">I finished this book on a plane. I was on a plane coming home from somewhere that I didn't belong and as we coasted onto the tarmac I felt a little like Jack Burden. He was never really comfortable in the shoes that he wore but was constantly striving to find the truth in things. He was looking for the truth while consistently doing the right even when it was hardest. Not to say that I am this all knowing altruistic seeker of truth in all things, quite the opposite, but coming from somewhere I d</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5072401516128592987">I finished this book on a plane. I was on a plane coming home from somewhere that I didn't belong and as we coasted onto the tarmac I felt a little like Jack Burden. He was never really comfortable in the shoes that he wore but was constantly striving to find the truth in things. He was looking for the truth while consistently doing the right even when it was hardest. Not to say that I am this all knowing altruistic seeker of truth in all things, quite the opposite, but coming from somewhere I didn't fit and into where I did I felt a connection with this narrator. I came to know and love Jack for his weaknesses and his strengths. Penn Warren created the single most honest, true and beautiful characters complete with faults and stupidity; I loved him. Unlike Burden, my own trip west was not to distance myself from what I couldn't bear but rather face my own "great twitch" head on and I felt the cleansing that his flight out west must have felt like. <br /><br />There is good and evil and I am aware some people think there is no good and evil but Penn Warren's contrast between Stark and Adam Stanton is a beautiful example of such. It seemed like so many of the characters had their own opposite. Each character had it's own alter ego, good/evil twin within another character as well as a little original sin thrown in there for shits and giggles. <br /><br />The female characters were a little to easily categorized though. All the archetypes were present. The devoted wife, the seductress, the angel, the smart ass. They were well developed but just in need of a little more reality. <br /><br />Brilliant, poetic, fatalistic, riveting and to top of my favorites. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5072401516128592987">...more</a>

          </span>

      <a href="/user/show/2190708-gary" class="user" title="Gary">Gary</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer54696447">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12514020364509803274">Ok......What did i think?? I wish I had read this book a loooooooooooongggggg time ago....... but maybe it was time to read it now. I think every American , whether Democrat, Republican, Independent, or I don't give a shit party, should read it..... It's a very modern topical novel to read now about how corruption can ruin a person,because we have to be right about everything........ instead of trying to work together for the better welfare of <br /><br />"everyone" in this country.<br /><br /><br />It's a book that makes yo</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12514020364509803274">Ok......What did i think?? I wish I had read this book a loooooooooooongggggg time ago....... but maybe it was time to read it now. I think every American , whether Democrat, Republican, Independent, or I don't give a shit party, should read it..... It's a very modern topical novel to read now about how corruption can ruin a person,because we have to be right about everything........ instead of trying to work together for the better welfare of <br /><br />"everyone" in this country.<br /><br /><br />It's a book that makes you realize not one way of thinking is correct. That being closed minded is the wrong path to take, that being a politician is not easy,and it's not necessarily rewarding,and it changes you into a person that maybe you don't really want to be. So many people say and act a certain way because they are afraid of what others think, but may act differently when left to their own devices..... to me that's what this novel points out.....at times the details were tedious, but then those details played a major role in the plot later.....as the plot thickened.<br /><br /><br />I came away from this novel relieved it was finally over, but then again sad to see it end. I see myself reading it again someday.<br /><br />I also found myself laughing outloud quite often at the humor,and the way southern people act..... cause this is a southern story,but it's sentiments hit all of us......<br /><br /><br />That is why I consider this , up to this point in life, of what I have read thus far, the greatest American political novel that I've ever read.<br /><br />Bravo, Robert! I wish you were still alive so I could attend your booksigning, and shake your hand....cause, dude....you rock!<br /><br /><br />Read it!<br /><br /></span>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/256749645" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5297390-mike-hart" class="left imgcol" title="Mike Hart"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1331237780p2/5297390.jpg" alt="Mike Hart" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/256749645?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 20, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5297390-mike-hart" class="user" title="Mike Hart">Mike Hart</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/5527.All_the_King_s_Men" title="Paperback 9780156004800" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer256749645">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15765780326144648417">Read this passage:<br /><br />A woman only laughs that way a few times in her life. A woman only laughs that way when something has touched her way down in the very quick of her being and the happiness just wells out as natural as breath and the first jonquils and mountain brooks. When a woman laughs that way it always does something to you. It does not matter what kind of a face she has got either. You hear that laugh and feel that you have grasped a clean and beautiful truth. You feel that way because tha</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15765780326144648417">Read this passage:<br /><br />A woman only laughs that way a few times in her life. A woman only laughs that way when something has touched her way down in the very quick of her being and the happiness just wells out as natural as breath and the first jonquils and mountain brooks. When a woman laughs that way it always does something to you. It does not matter what kind of a face she has got either. You hear that laugh and feel that you have grasped a clean and beautiful truth. You feel that way because that laugh is a revelation. It is a great impersonal sincerity. It is a spray of dewy blossom from the great central stalk of All Being, and the woman’s name and address hasn’t got a damn thing to do with it. Therefore, that laugh cannot be faked. If a woman could learn to fake it she would make Nell Gwyn and Pompadour look like a couple of Campfire Girls wearing bifocals and ground-gripper shoes and with bands on their teeth. She could set all society by the ears. For all any man really wants is to hear a woman laugh like that. <br /><br />Does the novel read that well in its entirety? Of course not. But the undulations of prose make sections like this so much more powerful. We read for entertainment. We read for escape. We read to better understand ourselves. And, sometimes, we read a book, and it changes us. People will comment heavily on the political nature of All the King's Men. It is, after all, a book about politics. But it is also a book about love, about loss, and about being a man. It is a book that made me realize how much I miss hearing that laugh. And now it's time to go and find that laugh once more.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15765780326144648417">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_345179028">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/345179028" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3816725-larry-bassett" class="left imgcol" title="Larry Bassett"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1390874855p2/3816725.jpg" alt="Larry Bassett" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/345179028?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 09, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3816725-larry-bassett" class="user" title="Larry Bassett">Larry Bassett</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/915849.All_the_King_s_Men" title="Hardcover 9780151047727" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3816725-larry-bassett?shelf=historical-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3816725-larry-bassett?shelf=political" class="actionLinkLite">political</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer345179028">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6804273425819488676">I like politics. Or maybe it would be more accurate to say that I have been involved with politics for a long time at a lot of different levels. Mostly my heart is with third party politics. This book has a special interest to me although I have never read it. The title is very familiar to me but I can’t say exactly why that is.<br /><br />I have come to believe that anyone who is successful in politics and has been elected is probably someone who is intellectually dishonest. After all, can you imagine some</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6804273425819488676">I like politics. Or maybe it would be more accurate to say that I have been involved with politics for a long time at a lot of different levels. Mostly my heart is with third party politics. This book has a special interest to me although I have never read it. The title is very familiar to me but I can’t say exactly why that is.<br /><br />I have come to believe that anyone who is successful in politics and has been elected is probably someone who is intellectually dishonest. After all, can you imagine someone being elected if s/he was honest about her/his beliefs? Politics is about double talk and bull shit and cronies and money and deals with the devil. But a politician would never say any of that. The goal is to be elected and then to stay in office. Willie Stark is a master of dirty, power politics.<br /><br />Call me a pessimist about politics. So I start <em>All the King’s Men</em> with a big chip on my shoulder and a “show me” attitude. Who is this Willie Stark, the Boss? And, who is Jack Burden, the man who is the “I” in the book, the narrator? <a title="Robert Penn Warren" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3736.Robert_Penn_Warren">Robert Penn Warren</a> tells us but it takes over 500 pages. I enjoyed almost all of those pages. It has been a while since I have enjoyed reading a book as much as this one.<br /><br />There are several different stories that go together to create this book. I enjoyed looking at the individual pieces of the puzzle and trying to find how they fit together. But this was a puzzle with a lot of pieces, more than I could always remember. So I used SparkNotes at the end of each chapter to help me look back at what I had already read: <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.sparknotes.com/lit/kingsmen/" target="_blank">http://www.sparknotes.com/lit/kingsmen/</a> . Yes, you may castigate me for cheating but there must be a reason ‘they’ made these notes and this might be one reason: to help my not-as-sharp-as-it-used-to-be brain out! Yes, that is it! I am implementing Willie’s philosophy: taking something bad and making something good out of it. Thanks Willie!<br /><br />This book makes me think about my dislike of achieving a good end via a bad means. It’s not just what you do but how you do it that counts. I have told myself that all of my adult life. I am looking inside myself to see if <em>All the King’s Men</em> has changed my mind even a little bit about that principle. Does this work? Politics is evil but it can do good things. No, I still think that the end does not justify the means.<br /><br />Here is a quiz after you have finished <em>All the King’s Men</em>. Actually the book asks you this one. Willie Stark was a great man. True or False?<br /><br />I have a hard time thinking that this book was published the same year I was born: 1946. It seems much more current even with the references to the 1920s and 1930s. Well, yes there are the spittoons and the telegrams and no DNA but the characters seem very real and up to date. <br /><br />This book gets an easy four stars from me. The book went slowly for me for a while at the start but it was not long before I was looking forward to reading more the next time. I liked all the building blocks that made up this book. Lots of good writing and things to think about. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6804273425819488676">...more</a>

          </span>

        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer186225861">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14935095119317105128">Robert Penn Warren's, All the Kings Men won the 1947 Pulitzer prize, and could also have won that prize in the next three years. <br /><br />Is this 400 pages of poetic prose or a great epic prosaic poem? This work would make a great primer for college English lit majors, I think Warren used every literary device and may have made up some more. <br /><br />And like so many master performances of art or sport, he makes it look effortless, he makes it look easy. This was like watching Joe DiMaggio glide across the outfie</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14935095119317105128">Robert Penn Warren's, All the Kings Men won the 1947 Pulitzer prize, and could also have won that prize in the next three years.? <br /><br />Is this 400 pages of poetic prose or a great epic prosaic poem? This work would make a great primer for college English lit majors, I think Warren used every literary device and may have made up some more.? <br /><br />And like so many master performances of art or sport, he makes it look effortless, he makes it look easy. This was like watching Joe DiMaggio glide across the outfield or Ted Williams at the plate, or Led Zeppelin on stage, this was swaggering virtuosity.? <br /><br />Telling the fictionalized account of Huey Long, Warren goes on to create the great American novel. Though the setting is politics, the book is not really about politics, the political stage is just a vehicle by which Warren explores a multi-layered, complicated series of thematic, interwoven observations on Western Civilization, and particularly our American chapter in that saga. <br /><br />This is periodically correct and yet timeless. Beautifully written and absolutely brilliant.<br /><br /><img width="400" height="400" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1430395659i/14709223._SX540_.jpg" /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_5537538">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/5537538" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/313009-andrew" class="left imgcol" title="Andrew"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1443655909p2/313009.jpg" alt="Andrew" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/5537538?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 02, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/313009-andrew" class="user" title="Andrew">Andrew</a>

          added it


          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/5527.All_the_King_s_Men" title="Paperback 9780156004800" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer5537538">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6091772995725735335">All the King’s Men is often promoted as a novel about politics, occasionally even the quintessential novel of American politics. While I did enjoy the portrait of Willie Stark as an archetype political boss, more interesting, to me, is the struggle of the narrator, Jack Burden, to overcome his nihilistic doubts in the face of a world governed by power. Jack claims to overcome his nihilism (“the Great Twitch”) by coming to an understanding of the morality of his own life (the personal and inter-p</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6091772995725735335">All the King’s Men is often promoted as a novel about politics, occasionally even the quintessential novel of American politics. While I did enjoy the portrait of Willie Stark as an archetype political boss, more interesting, to me, is the struggle of the narrator, Jack Burden, to overcome his nihilistic doubts in the face of a world governed by power. Jack claims to overcome his nihilism (“the Great Twitch”) by coming to an understanding of the morality of his own life (the personal and inter-personal) in relation to the ethical valuations of history (on the stage of the world).<br /><br />So I read the larger narrative of the book in the light of Jack’s search for meaning, rather than seeing Jack as merely a narrator to the story of Willie Stark. From this perspective I think All the King’s Men tells a compelling story of Stark as a politician who, despite by all outward appearances of being driven merely by a quest for power, can in fact believe in the righteousness of his actions. He accomplishes this through an elaborate scheme of means-end reasoning and a vision of purpose that captivates or even short-circuits his own moral calculus. Willie Stark constructs a narrative of meaning through his campaign for a hospital that will serve all people, regardless of income, and is not mired in the exigencies of political reality that becomes for him a trump card in any consideration of the morality of his actions.<br /><br />The ethically pious and occasionally self-righteous Doctor Adam Stanton serves as a foil to Stark, with their lives ultimately meeting in a tragic end as though destined by their inability to grasp the ethical possibilities of their world that the narrator, Jack Burden, eventually realizes. Adam’s attempt to live ethically through nonparticipation with evil and political power is sacrificed with the realization of his father’s ethical shortcomings; he then steps into the world of power by agreeing to oversee the construction of Willie Stark’s hospital (creating odd parallel between the two in that the hospital serves for both of them as the symbolic touchstone for an ethical life). Yet this critical step, away from a sanctified idealism into the practical world of compromise leads eventually to his tragic confrontation with Willie Stark. In the end, his defining action is not aimed at any practical end but is rather either an emotionally-driven strike against the objectification of his own (and his sister’s) moral compromise. The reader is left to ponder whether this outcome is this simply the natural result of his step away from a “kingdom of ends” in agreeing to join Stark’s hospital or a product of his inability to cope with the fallen nature of humanity.<br /><br />That’s what I got out of it anyway. For a more critical view highlighting literary shortcomings and racial and gender issues, read “All the King’s Men—A Case of Misreading?” by Joyce Carol Oats in the New York Times Book Review.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6091772995725735335">...more</a>

          </span>
